<p>A <b>video game</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Electronic_game" title="Electronic game">electronic game</a> that involves human interaction with a <a href="/wiki/User_interface" title="User interface">user interface</a> to generate visual feedback on a <a href="/wiki/Video" title="Video">video device</a> such as a TV screen or computer monitor. The word <i>video</i> in <i>video game</i> traditionally referred to a <a href="/wiki/Raster_scan" title="Raster scan">raster</a> display device, but as of the 2000s, it implies any type of <a href="/wiki/Display_device" title="Display device">display device</a> that can produce two- or three-dimensional images. Some theorists categorize video games as an <a href="/wiki/Video_games_as_an_art_form" title="Video games as an art form">art form</a>, but this designation is controversial.</p>
<p>The electronic systems used to play video games are known as platforms; examples of these are personal computers and <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">video game consoles</a>. These platforms range from large <a href="/wiki/Mainframe_computer" title="Mainframe computer">mainframe computers</a> to small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Handheld_device" title="Handheld device">handheld computing devices</a>. Specialized video games such as <a href="/wiki/Arcade_game" title="Arcade game">arcade games</a>, in which the video game components are housed in a large, coin-operated chassis, while common in the 1980s in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_arcade" title="Video arcade">video arcades</a>, have gradually declined in use due to the widespread availability of affordable home video game consoles (e.g., <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_4" title="PlayStation 4">PlayStation 4</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xbox_One" title="Xbox One">Xbox One</a>) and video games on desktop and laptop computers and <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Input_device" title="Input device">input device</a> used for games, the <a href="/wiki/Game_controller" title="Game controller">game controller</a>, varies across platforms. Common controllers include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamepads" title="Gamepads">gamepads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joystick" title="Joystick">joysticks</a>, mouses, keyboards, the <a href="/wiki/Touchscreen" title="Touchscreen">touchscreens</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mobile_device" title="Mobile device">mobile devices</a> and buttons. Players typically view the game on a video screen or <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a> and there are often game sounds from <a href="/wiki/Loudspeaker" title="Loudspeaker">loudspeakers</a>. Some games in the 2000s include <a href="/wiki/Haptic_technology" title="Haptic technology">haptic</a>, vibration-creating effects, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Force_feedback" title="Force feedback">force feedback</a> <a href="/wiki/Peripheral" title="Peripheral">peripherals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality" title="Virtual reality">virtual reality</a> headsets. In the 2010s, the <a href="/wiki/Video_game_industry" title="Video game industry">video game industry</a> is of increasing commercial importance, with growth driven particularly by the emerging Asian markets and <a href="/wiki/Mobile_game" title="Mobile game">mobile games</a>, which are played on <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>. As of 2015, video games generated sales of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USD" title="USD">USD</a> 74 billion annually worldwide, and were the third-largest segment in the U.S. entertainment market, behind broadcast and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cable_TV" title="Cable TV">cable TV</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_video_games" title="History of video games">History of video games</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_history_of_video_games" title="Early history of video games">Early history of video games</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tennis_for_Two_-_Modern_recreation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Tennis_for_Two_-_Modern_recreation.jpg/220px-Tennis_for_Two_-_Modern_recreation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Tennis_for_Two_-_Modern_recreation.jpg/330px-Tennis_for_Two_-_Modern_recreation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Tennis_for_Two_-_Modern_recreation.jpg/440px-Tennis_for_Two_-_Modern_recreation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tennis_for_Two_-_Modern_recreation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Tennis for Two</i>, an early <a href="/wiki/Analog_computer" title="Analog computer">analog computer</a> game that used an <a href="/wiki/Oscilloscope" title="Oscilloscope">oscilloscope</a> for a display</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Early games used interactive electronic devices with various display formats. The earliest example is from 1947â€”a "<a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" title="Cathode ray tube">Cathode ray tube</a> Amusement Device" was filed for a patent on 25 January 1947, by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_T._Goldsmith_Jr." title="Thomas T. Goldsmith Jr.">Thomas T. Goldsmith Jr.</a> and Estle Ray Mann, and issued on 14 December 1948, as U.S. Patent 2455992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Inspired by <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> display technology, it consisted of an analog device that allowed a user to control a vector-drawn dot on the screen to simulate a missile being fired at targets, which were drawings fixed to the screen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PongStory_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PongStory-2">[2]</a></sup> Other early examples include: The <a href="/wiki/Nimrod_(computing)" title="Nimrod (computing)">Nimrod</a> computer at the 1951 <a href="/wiki/Festival_of_Britain" title="Festival of Britain">Festival of Britain</a>; <i><a href="/wiki/OXO" title="OXO">OXO</a></i> a <a href="/wiki/Tic-tac-toe" title="Tic-tac-toe">tic-tac-toe</a> Computer game by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.S._Douglas" title="A.S. Douglas">Alexander S. Douglas</a> for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EDSAC" title="EDSAC">EDSAC</a> in 1952; <i><a href="/wiki/Tennis_for_Two" title="Tennis for Two">Tennis for Two</a></i>, an electronic interactive game engineered by <a href="/wiki/William_Higinbotham" title="William Higinbotham">William Higinbotham</a> in 1958; <i><a href="/wiki/Spacewar!" title="Spacewar!">Spacewar!</a></i>, written by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a> students Martin Graetz, Steve Russell, and Wayne Wiitanen's on a DEC <a href="/wiki/PDP-1" title="PDP-1">PDP-1</a> computer in 1961; and the hit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ping_pong" title="Ping pong">ping pong</a>-style <i><a href="/wiki/Pong" title="Pong">Pong</a></i>, a 1972 game by <a href="/wiki/Atari" title="Atari">Atari</a>. Each game used different means of display: NIMROD used a panel of lights to play the game of <a href="/wiki/Nim" title="Nim">Nim</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> OXO used a graphical display to play tic-tac-toe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <i>Tennis for Two</i> used an oscilloscope to display a side view of a tennis court,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PongStory_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-PongStory-2">[2]</a></sup> and <i>Spacewar!</i> used the <a href="/wiki/Digital_Equipment_Corporation" title="Digital Equipment Corporation">DEC</a> PDP-1's vector display to have two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spaceships" title="Spaceships">spaceships</a> battle each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nolan_Bushnell_-_Game_Developers_Conference_Online_2011_(4).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Nolan_Bushnell_-_Game_Developers_Conference_Online_2011_%284%29.jpg/220px-Nolan_Bushnell_-_Game_Developers_Conference_Online_2011_%284%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Nolan_Bushnell_-_Game_Developers_Conference_Online_2011_%284%29.jpg/330px-Nolan_Bushnell_-_Game_Developers_Conference_Online_2011_%284%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Nolan_Bushnell_-_Game_Developers_Conference_Online_2011_%284%29.jpg/440px-Nolan_Bushnell_-_Game_Developers_Conference_Online_2011_%284%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nolan_Bushnell_-_Game_Developers_Conference_Online_2011_(4).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Nolan_Bushnell" title="Nolan Bushnell">Nolan Bushnell</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Game_Developers_Conference" title="Game Developers Conference">Game Developers Conference</a> in 2011</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1971, <i><a href="/wiki/Computer_Space" title="Computer Space">Computer Space</a></i>, created by <a href="/wiki/Nolan_Bushnell" title="Nolan Bushnell">Nolan Bushnell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ted_Dabney" title="Ted Dabney">Ted Dabney</a>, was the first commercially sold, coin-operated video game. It used a black-and-white <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a> for its display, and the computer system was made of <a href="/wiki/7400_series" title="7400 series">74 series</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transistor-transistor_logic" title="Transistor-transistor logic">TTL</a> <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">chips</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The game was featured in the 1973 <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_film" title="Science fiction film">science fiction film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Soylent_Green" title="Soylent Green">Soylent Green</a></i>. <i>Computer Space</i> was followed in 1972 by the <a href="/wiki/Magnavox_Odyssey" title="Magnavox Odyssey">Magnavox Odyssey</a>, the first home console. Modeled after a late 1960s prototype console developed by <a href="/wiki/Ralph_H._Baer" title="Ralph H. Baer">Ralph H. Baer</a> called the "Brown Box", it also used a standard television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PongStory_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-PongStory-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> These were followed by two versions of <a href="/wiki/Atari,_Inc." title="Atari, Inc.">Atari</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Pong" title="Pong">Pong</a></i>; an <a href="/wiki/Arcade_game" title="Arcade game">arcade</a> version in 1972 and a home version in 1975 that dramatically increased video game popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBS-GameRev-Time_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-GameRev-Time-8">[8]</a></sup> The commercial success of <i>Pong</i> led numerous other companies to develop <i>Pong</i> clones and their own systems, spawning the <a href="/wiki/Video_game_industry" title="Video game industry">video game industry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InformIT_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-InformIT-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>A flood of <i>Pong</i> clones eventually led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_game_crash_of_1977" title="Video game crash of 1977">video game crash of 1977</a>, which came to an end with the mainstream success of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taito_Corporation" title="Taito Corporation">Taito</a>'s 1978 <a href="/wiki/Shooter_game" title="Shooter game">shooter game</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Invaders" title="Space Invaders">Space Invaders</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittaker-122_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whittaker-122-10">[10]</a></sup> marking the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_arcade_video_games" title="Golden age of arcade video games">golden age of arcade video games</a> and inspiring dozens of manufacturers to enter the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittaker-122_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Whittaker-122-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kent-500_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kent-500-11">[11]</a></sup> The game inspired arcade machines to become prevalent in mainstream locations such as shopping malls, traditional storefronts, restaurants, and convenience stores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The game also became the subject of numerous articles and stories on television and in newspapers and magazines, establishing <a href="/wiki/Video_game_culture" title="Video game culture">video gaming</a> as a rapidly growing mainstream hobby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eg_2_36_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-eg_2_36-14">[14]</a></sup> <i>Space Invaders</i> was soon licensed for the <a href="/wiki/Atari_2600" title="Atari 2600">Atari VCS</a> (later known as Atari 2600), becoming the first "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Killer_app" title="Killer app">killer app</a>" and quadrupling the console's sales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RG-41_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-RG-41-15">[15]</a></sup> This helped Atari recover from their earlier losses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montfort-66_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montfort-66-16">[16]</a></sup> and in turn the Atari VCS revived the home video game market during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_video_game_consoles_(second_generation)" title="History of video game consoles (second generation)">second generation of consoles</a>, up until the <a href="/wiki/North_American_video_game_crash_of_1983" title="North American video game crash of 1983">North American video game crash of 1983</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The home video game industry was revitalized shortly afterwards by the widespread success of the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Entertainment_System" title="Nintendo Entertainment System">Nintendo Entertainment System</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> which marked a shift in the dominance of the video game industry from the United States to Japan during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_video_game_consoles_(third_generation)" title="History of video game consoles (third generation)">third generation of consoles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cgw_50_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-cgw_50-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Platforms">Platforms</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_video_games" title="History of video games">History of video games</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:6gencons.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="780" data-file-width="2298" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/6gencons.JPG/220px-6gencons.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/6gencons.JPG/330px-6gencons.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/6gencons.JPG/440px-6gencons.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:6gencons.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Different <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">video game consoles</a> from left to right: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sega_Dreamcast" title="Sega Dreamcast">Sega Dreamcast</a>, <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_2" title="PlayStation 2">PlayStation 2</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nintendo_Gamecube" title="Nintendo Gamecube">Nintendo Gamecube</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Xbox_(console)" title="Xbox (console)">Xbox</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The term "platform" refers to the specific combination of <a href="/wiki/Electronic_component" title="Electronic component">electronic components</a> or computer hardware which, in conjunction with software, allows a video game to operate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-platformdef_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-platformdef-20">[20]</a></sup> The term "system" is also commonly used. The distinctions below are not always clear and there may be games that bridge one or more platforms. In addition to personal computers, there are other devices which have the ability to play games but are not dedicated video game machines, such as <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PDA" title="PDA">PDAs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graphing_calculator" title="Graphing calculator">graphing calculators</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="PC">PC</span></h4>
<p>In common use a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personal_computer_game" title="Personal computer game">PC game</a>" refers to a form of media that involves a player interacting with a <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computer</a> connected to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_monitor" title="Video monitor">video monitor</a>. <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">Personal computers</a> are not dedicated game platforms, so there may be differences running the same game in different hardwares, also the openness allows some features to developers like reduced software cost,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> increased flexibility, increased innovation, emulation, creation of modifications ("<a href="/wiki/Mod_(video_gaming)" title="Mod (video gaming)">mods</a>"), open hosting for <a href="/wiki/Online_game" title="Online game">online gaming</a> (in which a person plays a video game with people who are in a different household) and others.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Console">Console</span></h4>
<p>A "<a href="/wiki/Console_game" title="Console game">console game</a>" is played on a specialized electronic device that connects to a common <a href="/wiki/Television_set" title="Television set">television set</a> or <a href="/wiki/Composite_monitor" title="Composite monitor">composite video monitor</a>, unlike <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">PCs</a>, a console is a dedicated platform, it is manufactured by a specific company. Usually runs only games developed for it, or games from other platform made by the same company, but never its direct competitor, even if the same game is available on different platforms. It often comes with a specific <a href="/wiki/Game_controller" title="Game controller">game controller</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Handheld">Handheld</span></h4>
<p>A "<a href="/wiki/Handheld_game_console" title="Handheld game console">handheld</a>" gaming device is a small, self-contained electronic device that is portable and can be held in a user's hands. It features the console, a small screen, speakers and buttons, <a href="/wiki/Joystick" title="Joystick">joystick</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Game_controller" title="Game controller">game controllers</a> in a single unit. Like consoles, handhelds are dedicated platforms, and share almost the same characteristics. Handheld hardware usually is less powerful than PC or console hardwares. Some handheld games from the late 1970s and early 1980s could only play one game. In the 1990s and 2000s, a number of handheld games used cartridges, which enabled them to be used to play many different games.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arcade">Arcade</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_gun_survival_horror_arcade_game.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Light_gun_survival_horror_arcade_game.jpg/200px-Light_gun_survival_horror_arcade_game.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Light_gun_survival_horror_arcade_game.jpg/300px-Light_gun_survival_horror_arcade_game.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Light_gun_survival_horror_arcade_game.jpg/400px-Light_gun_survival_horror_arcade_game.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_gun_survival_horror_arcade_game.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A horror-themed arcade game in which players use a <a href="/wiki/Light_gun" title="Light gun">light gun</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Arcade_game" title="Arcade game">Arcade game</a>" generally refers to a game played on an even more specialized type of electronic device that is typically designed to play only one game and is encased in a special, large coin-operated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcade_cabinet" title="Arcade cabinet">cabinet</a> which has one built-in console, controllers (joystick, buttons, etc.), a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CRT_screen" title="CRT screen">CRT screen</a>, and audio amplifier and speakers. Arcade games often have brightly painted logos and images relating to the theme of the game. While most arcade games are housed in a vertical cabinet, which the user typically stands in front of to play, some arcade games use a tabletop approach, in which the display screen is housed in a table-style cabinet with a see-through table top. With table-top games, the users typically sit to play. In the 1990s and 2000s, some arcade games offered players a choice of multiple games. In the 1980s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_arcade" title="Video arcade">video arcades</a> were businesses in which game players could use a number of arcade video games. In the 2010s, there are far fewer video arcades, but some <a href="/wiki/Movie_theater" title="Movie theater">movie theaters</a> and family entertainment centers still have them.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Web_browser">Web browser</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browser</a> has also established itself as platform in its own right in the 2000s, while providing a <a href="/wiki/Cross-platform" title="Cross-platform">cross-platform</a> environment for video games designed to be played on a wide spectrum of hardware from personal computers and <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablet computers</a> to <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>. This in turn has generated new terms to qualify classes of web browser-based games. These games may be identified based on the website that they appear, such as with "<a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a>" games. Others are named based on the programming platform used to develop them, such as <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Flash" title="Adobe Flash">Flash</a> games.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genres">Genres</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Video_game_genre" title="Video game genre">Video game genre</a></div>
<p>A video game, like most other forms of media, may be categorized into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genres" title="Genres">genres</a>. Video game genres are used to categorize video games based on their gameplay interaction rather than <a href="/wiki/Computer_graphics" title="Computer graphics">visual</a> or <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">narrative</a> differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apperley_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Apperley-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gamasutra_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gamasutra-23">[23]</a></sup> A video game genre is defined by a set of gameplay challenges and are classified independent of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Setting_(fiction)" title="Setting (fiction)">setting</a> or game-world content, unlike other works of fiction such as <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">films</a> or <a href="/wiki/Book" title="Book">books</a>. For example, a <a href="/wiki/Shooter_game" title="Shooter game">shooter game</a> is still a shooter game, regardless of whether it takes place in a fantasy world or in outer space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundamentals_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-fundamentals-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because genres are dependent on content for definition, genres have changed and evolved as newer styles of video games have come into existence. Ever advancing technology and production values related to video game development have fostered more lifelike and complex games which have in turn introduced or enhanced genre possibilities (e.g., virtual pets), pushed the boundaries of existing video gaming or in some cases add new possibilities in play (such as that seen with titles specifically designed for devices like Sony's <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_Eye" title="PlayStation Eye">EyeToy</a>). Some genres represent combinations of others, such as <a href="/wiki/Massively_multiplayer_online_role-playing_game" title="Massively multiplayer online role-playing game">massively multiplayer online role-playing games</a>, or, more commonly, MMORPGs. It is also common to see higher level genre terms that are collective in nature across all other genres such as with action, music/rhythm or horror-themed video games.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classifications">Classifications</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Casual_games">Casual games</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Casual_game" title="Casual game">Casual game</a></div>
<p>Casual games derive their name from their ease of accessibility, simple to understand gameplay and quick to grasp rule sets. Additionally, casual games frequently support the ability to jump in and out of play on demand. Casual games as a format existed long before the term was coined and include video games such as Solitaire or Minesweeper which can commonly be found pre-installed with many versions of the <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> operating system. Examples of <a href="/wiki/Video_game_genre" title="Video game genre">genres</a> within this category are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Match_three" title="Match three">match three</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hidden_object" title="Hidden object">hidden object</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_Management_(video_game_genre)" title="Time Management (video game genre)">time management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puzzle_video_game" title="Puzzle video game">puzzle</a> or many of the <a href="/wiki/Tower_defense" title="Tower defense">tower defense</a> style games. Casual games are generally available through <a href="/wiki/App_store" title="App store">app stores</a> and online retailers such as <a href="/wiki/PopCap_Games" title="PopCap Games">PopCap</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zylom" title="Zylom">Zylom</a> and <a href="/wiki/GameHouse" title="GameHouse">GameHouse</a> or provided for free play through <a href="/wiki/Web_portal" title="Web portal">web portals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Newgrounds" title="Newgrounds">Newgrounds</a>. While casual games are most commonly played on personal computers, phones or tablets, they can also be found on many of the on-line console system download services (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_Network" title="PlayStation Network">PlayStation Network</a>, <a href="/wiki/WiiWare" title="WiiWare">WiiWare</a> or <a href="/wiki/Xbox_Live" title="Xbox Live">Xbox Live</a>).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Serious_games">Serious games</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Serious_game" title="Serious game">Serious game</a></div>
<p>Serious games are games that are designed primarily to convey information or a learning experience of some sort to the player. Some serious games may even fail to qualify as a video game in the traditional sense of the term. Educational software does not typically fall under this category (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Touch_typing" title="Touch typing">touch typing</a> tutors, language learning, etc.) and the primary distinction would appear to be based on the title's primary goal as well as target age demographics. As with the other categories, this description is more of a guideline than a rule.</p>
<p>Serious games are games generally made for reasons beyond simple entertainment and as with the core and casual games may include works from any given genre, although some such as <a href="/wiki/Exergaming" title="Exergaming">exergames</a>, <a href="/wiki/Educational_game" title="Educational game">educational games</a>, or propaganda games may have a higher representation in this group due to their subject matter. These games are typically designed to be played by professionals as part of a specific job or for skill set improvement. They can also be created to convey social-political awareness on a specific subject.</p>
<p>One of the longest running serious games franchises would be <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Flight_Simulator" title="Microsoft Flight Simulator">Microsoft Flight Simulator</a> first published in 1982 under that name. The United States military uses <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality" title="Virtual reality">virtual reality</a> based simulations, such as <a href="/wiki/VBS1" title="VBS1">VBS1</a> for training exercises,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> as do a growing number of first responder roles (e.g., police, fire fighter, EMT).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> One example of a non-game environment utilized as a platform for serious game development would be the virtual world of <i><a href="/wiki/Second_Life" title="Second Life">Second Life</a></i>, which is currently used by several United States governmental departments (e.g., <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">NOAA</a>, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">JPL</a>), Universities (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Ohio_University" title="Ohio University">Ohio University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a>) for educational and remote learning programs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and businesses (e.g., <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cisco_Systems" title="Cisco Systems">Cisco Systems</a>) for meetings and training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tactical_media" title="Tactical media">Tactical media</a> in video games plays a crucial role in making a statement or conveying a message on important relevant issues. This form of media allows for a broader audience to be able to receive and gain access to certain information that otherwise may not have reached such people. An example of tactical media in video games would be <a href="/wiki/Newsgame" title="Newsgame">newsgames</a>. These are short games related to contemporary events designed to illustrate a point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DiGRA_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-DiGRA-30">[30]</a></sup> For example, Take Action Games is a game studio collective that was co-founded by Susana Ruiz and has made successful serious games. Some of these games include <i><a href="/wiki/Darfur_is_Dying" title="Darfur is Dying">Darfur is Dying</a></i>, <i>Finding Zoe</i>, and <i>In The Balance</i>. All of these games bring awareness to important issues and events in an intelligent and well thought out manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_games">Educational games</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Educational_video_games" title="Educational video games">Educational video games</a> and <a href="/wiki/Educational_software" title="Educational software">Educational software</a></div>
<p>On 23 September 2009, U.S. President Barack Obama launched a campaign called "Educate to Innovate" aimed at improving the technological, mathematical, scientific and engineering abilities of American students. This campaign states that it plans to harness the power of interactive games to help achieve the goal of students excelling in these departments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> This campaign has stemmed into many new opportunities for the video game realm and has contributed to many new competitions. Some of these competitions include the Stem National Video Game Competition and the <a href="/wiki/Imagine_Cup" title="Imagine Cup">Imagine Cup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Both of these examples are events that bring a focus to relevant and important current issues that are able to be addressed in the sense of video games to educate and spread knowledge in a new form of media. www.NobelPrize.org uses games to entice the user to learn about information pertaining to the Nobel prize achievements while engaging in a fun to play video game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> There are many different types and styles of educational games all the way from counting to spelling to games for kids and games for adults. Some other games do not have any particular targeted audience in mind and intended to simply educate or inform whoever views or plays the game.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controllers">Controllers</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Game_controller" title="Game controller">Game controller</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SNES-Controller.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2080" data-file-width="3700" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/SNES-Controller.jpg/220px-SNES-Controller.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/SNES-Controller.jpg/330px-SNES-Controller.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/SNES-Controller.jpg/440px-SNES-Controller.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SNES-Controller.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A North American <a href="/wiki/Super_Nintendo_Entertainment_System" title="Super Nintendo Entertainment System">Super NES</a> game controller from the early 1990s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Video game can use several types of input devices to translate human actions to a game, the most common game controllers are keyboard and mouse for "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personal_computer_game" title="Personal computer game">PC games</a>, consoles usually come with specific <a href="/wiki/Gamepad" title="Gamepad">gamepads</a>, handheld consoles have built in buttons. Other game controllers are commonly used for specific games like <a href="/wiki/Racing_wheel" title="Racing wheel">racing wheels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Light_gun" title="Light gun">light guns</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dance_pad" title="Dance pad">dance pads</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_camera" title="Digital camera">Digital cameras</a> can also be used as game controllers capturing movements of the body of the player.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_development" title="Game development">Game development</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_game_industry_practices" title="Video game industry practices">Video game industry practices</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Virtual-camera-system.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="322" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Virtual-camera-system.png/220px-Virtual-camera-system.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/Virtual-camera-system.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Virtual-camera-system.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Developers use various tools to create video games. Here an editor is fine-tuning the <a href="/wiki/Virtual_camera_system" title="Virtual camera system">virtual camera system</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Video game development and authorship, much like any other form of entertainment, is frequently a cross-disciplinary field. <a href="/wiki/Video_game_developer" title="Video game developer">Video game developers</a>, as employees within this industry are commonly referred, primarily include <a href="/wiki/Game_programmer" title="Game programmer">programmers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graphic_design" title="Graphic design">graphic designers</a>. Over the years this has expanded to include almost every type of skill that one might see prevalent in the creation of any movie or television program, including <a href="/wiki/Sound_design" title="Sound design">sound designers</a>, musicians, and other technicians; as well as skills that are specific to video games, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_designer" title="Game designer">game designer</a>. All of these are managed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_producer" title="Game producer">producers</a>.</p>
<p>In the early days of the industry, it was more common for a single person to manage all of the roles needed to create a video game. As platforms have become more complex and powerful in the type of material they can present, larger teams have been needed to generate all of the art, programming, cinematography, and more. This is not to say that the age of the "one-man shop" is gone, as this is still sometimes found in the casual gaming and handheld markets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-numberofdevs_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-numberofdevs-37">[37]</a></sup> where smaller games are prevalent due to technical limitations such as limited <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAM" title="RAM">RAM</a> or lack of dedicated 3D graphics rendering capabilities on the target platform (e.g., some cellphones and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PDAs" title="PDAs">PDAs</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>With the growth of the size of development teams in the industry, the problem of cost has increased. Development studios need to be able to pay their staff a competitive wage in order to attract and retain the best talent, while publishers are constantly looking to keep costs down in order to maintain profitability on their investment. Typically, a video game console development team can range in sizes of anywhere from 5 to 50 people, with some teams exceeding 100. In May 2009, one game project was reported to have a development staff of 450.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The growth of team size combined with greater pressure to get completed projects into the market to begin recouping production costs has led to a greater occurrence of missed deadlines, rushed games and the release of unfinished products.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Downloadable_content">Downloadable content</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Downloadable_content" title="Downloadable content">Downloadable content</a></div>
<p>A phenomenon of additional game content at a later date, often for additional funds, began with digital video game distribution known as downloadable content (DLC). Developers can use <a href="/wiki/Digital_distribution" title="Digital distribution">digital distribution</a> to issue new storylines after the main game is released, such as <a href="/wiki/Rockstar_Games" title="Rockstar Games">Rockstar Games</a> with <i><a href="/wiki/Grand_Theft_Auto_IV" title="Grand Theft Auto IV">Grand Theft Auto IV</a></i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Grand_Theft_Auto:_The_Lost_and_Damned" title="Grand Theft Auto: The Lost and Damned">The Lost and Damned</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Grand_Theft_Auto:_The_Ballad_of_Gay_Tony" title="Grand Theft Auto: The Ballad of Gay Tony">The Ballad of Gay Tony</a></i>), or Bethesda with <i><a href="/wiki/Fallout_3" title="Fallout 3">Fallout 3</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Fallout_3_downloadable_content" title="Fallout 3 downloadable content">its expansions</a>. New gameplay modes can also become available, for instance, <i><a href="/wiki/Call_of_Duty" title="Call of Duty">Call of Duty</a></i> and its zombie modes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> a multiplayer mode for <i><a href="/wiki/Mushroom_Wars" title="Mushroom Wars">Mushroom Wars</a></i> or a higher difficulty level for <i><a href="/wiki/Metro:_Last_Light" title="Metro: Last Light">Metro: Last Light</a></i>. Smaller packages of DLC are also common, ranging from better in-game weapons (<i><a href="/wiki/Dead_Space_(series)" title="Dead Space (series)">Dead Space</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Just_Cause_2" title="Just Cause 2">Just Cause 2</a></i>), character outfits (<i><a href="/wiki/LittleBigPlanet" title="LittleBigPlanet">LittleBigPlanet</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Minecraft" title="Minecraft">Minecraft</a></i>), or new songs to perform (<i><a href="/wiki/SingStar" title="SingStar">SingStar</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rock_Band" title="Rock Band">Rock Band</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Guitar_Hero" title="Guitar Hero">Guitar Hero</a></i>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modifications">Modifications</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mod_(computer_gaming)" title="Mod (computer gaming)">Mod (computer gaming)</a></div>
<p>Many games produced for the PC are designed such that technically oriented consumers can modify the game. These <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mod_(computer_gaming)" title="Mod (computer gaming)">mods</a> can add an extra dimension of replayability and interest. Developers such as <a href="/wiki/Id_Software" title="Id Software">id Software</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valve_Corporation" title="Valve Corporation">Valve Corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crytek" title="Crytek">Crytek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bethesda_Softworks" title="Bethesda Softworks">Bethesda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epic_Games" title="Epic Games">Epic Games</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blizzard_Entertainment" title="Blizzard Entertainment">Blizzard Entertainment</a> ship their games with some of the development tools used to make the game, along with documentation to assist mod developers. The Internet provides an inexpensive medium to promote and distribute mods, and they may be a factor in the commercial success of some games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lombardi_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-lombardi-42">[42]</a></sup> This allows for the kind of success seen by popular mods such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Half-Life_(video_game)" title="Half-Life (video game)">Half-Life</a></i> mod <i><a href="/wiki/Counter-Strike_(video_game)" title="Counter-Strike (video game)">Counter-Strike</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cheating">Cheating</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cheating_(video_games)" title="Cheating (video games)">Cheating (video games)</a></div>
<p>Cheating in computer games may involve <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cheat_codes" title="Cheat codes">cheat codes</a> and hidden spots implemented by the game developers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WashPost_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-WashPost-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KonamiCode_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-KonamiCode-44">[44]</a></sup> modification of game code by third parties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AboutCheatCodes_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-AboutCheatCodes-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> or players exploiting a software glitch. Modifications are facilitated by either <a href="/wiki/Cheat_cartridge" title="Cheat cartridge">cheat cartridge</a> hardware or a software <a href="/wiki/Trainer_(games)" title="Trainer (games)">trainer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AboutCheatCodes_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-AboutCheatCodes-45">[45]</a></sup> Cheats usually make the game <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Difficulty_level" title="Difficulty level">easier</a> by providing an unlimited amount of some resource; for example weapons, health, or ammunition; or perhaps the ability to walk through walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KonamiCode_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-KonamiCode-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AboutCheatCodes_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-AboutCheatCodes-45">[45]</a></sup> Other cheats might give access to otherwise unplayable levels or provide unusual or amusing features, like altered game colors or other graphical appearances.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glitches">Glitches</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glitch" title="Glitch">Glitch</a></div>
<p>Software errors not detected by software testers during development can find their way into released versions of computer and video games. This may happen because the glitch only occurs under unusual circumstances in the game, was deemed too minor to correct, or because the game development was hurried to meet a publication deadline. Glitches can range from minor graphical errors to serious bugs that can delete saved data or cause the game to malfunction. In some cases publishers will release updates (referred to as <i>patches</i>) to repair glitches. Sometimes a glitch may be beneficial to the player; these are often referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploit_(online_gaming)" title="Exploit (online gaming)">exploits</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Easter_eggs">Easter eggs</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Easter_egg_(media)" title="Easter egg (media)">Easter eggs</a> are hidden messages or jokes left in games by developers that are not part of the main game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Easter eggs are secret responses that occur as a result of an undocumented set of commands. The results can vary from a simple printed message or image, to a page of programmer credits or a small videogame hidden inside an otherwise serious piece of software. Videogame <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cheat_codes" title="Cheat codes">cheat codes</a> are a specific type of Easter egg, in which entering a secret command will unlock special powers or new levels for the player.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolf_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolf-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patterns_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patterns-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory">Theory</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Game_studies" title="Game studies">Game studies</a></div>
<p>Although departments of computer science have been studying the technical aspects of video games for years, theories that examine games as an artistic medium are a relatively recent development in the humanities. The two most visible schools in this emerging field are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ludology" title="Ludology">ludology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Narratology" title="Narratology">narratology</a>. Narrativists approach video games in the context of what <a href="/wiki/Janet_Murray" title="Janet Murray">Janet Murray</a> calls "Cyberdrama". That is to say, their major concern is with video games as a storytelling medium, one that arises out of <a href="/wiki/Interactive_fiction" title="Interactive fiction">interactive fiction</a>. Murray puts video games in the context of the <a href="/wiki/Holodeck" title="Holodeck">Holodeck</a>, a fictional piece of technology from <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek" title="Star Trek">Star Trek</a></i>, arguing for the video game as a medium in which we get to become another person, and to act out in another world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray1998_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray1998-50">[50]</a></sup> This image of video games received early widespread popular support, and forms the basis of films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tron" title="Tron">Tron</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EXistenZ" title="EXistenZ">eXistenZ</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Starfighter" title="The Last Starfighter">The Last Starfighter</a></i>.</p>
<p>Ludologists break sharply and radically from this idea. They argue that a video game is first and foremost a game, which must be understood in terms of its rules, interface, and the concept of play that it deploys. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Espen_J._Aarseth" title="Espen J. Aarseth">Espen J. Aarseth</a> argues that, although games certainly have plots, characters, and aspects of traditional narratives, these aspects are incidental to gameplay. For example, Aarseth is critical of the widespread attention that narrativists have given to the heroine of the game <i><a href="/wiki/Tomb_Raider" title="Tomb Raider">Tomb Raider</a></i>, saying that "the dimensions of <a href="/wiki/Lara_Croft" title="Lara Croft">Lara Croft</a>'s body, already analyzed to death by <a href="/wiki/Film_theory" title="Film theory">film theorists</a>, are irrelevant to me as a player, because a different-looking body would not make me play differently... When I play, I don't even see her body, but see through it and past it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Simply put, ludologists reject traditional theories of art because they claim that the artistic and socially relevant qualities of a video game are primarily determined by the underlying set of rules, demands, and expectations imposed on the player.</p>
<p>While many games rely on <a href="/wiki/Emergence" title="Emergence">emergent principles</a>, video games commonly present simulated story worlds where emergent behavior occurs within the context of the game. The term "emergent narrative" has been used to describe how, in a simulated environment, storyline can be created simply by "what happens to the player."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> However, emergent behavior is not limited to sophisticated games. In general, any place where event-driven instructions occur for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_Intelligence" title="Artificial Intelligence">AI</a> in a game, emergent behavior will exist. For instance, take a racing game in which cars are programmed to avoid crashing, and they encounter an obstacle in the track: the cars might then maneuver to avoid the obstacle causing the cars behind them to slow and/or maneuver to accommodate the cars in front of them and the obstacle. The programmer never wrote code to specifically create a traffic jam, yet one now exists in the game.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emulation">Emulation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console_emulator" title="Video game console emulator">Video game console emulator</a></div>
<p>Emulators are software that allow games to run on a different platform from the original hardware by replicate the behavior of a console, they are found in <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">PCs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">consoles</a>. Emulators emerged in the early 1990s, they are generally used to play old games, <a href="/wiki/ROM_hacking" title="ROM hacking">hack existing games</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fan_translation_of_video_games" title="Fan translation of video games">translate unreleased games in an especific region</a> or add enhanced features to games like improved graphics, speed up or down, bypass regional lockouts, or online multiplayer support. In the United States, emulation without permission of the publisher is illegal.</p>
<p>Due to great demand, began to emerge emulation officially, the first case was the <a href="/wiki/Virtual_Console" title="Virtual Console">Virtual Console</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nintendo_Wii" title="Nintendo Wii">Nintendo Wii</a>, it was one of <a href="/wiki/Nintendo" title="Nintendo">Nintendo</a>'s shares to prevent piracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In November 2015, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> lanched backwards compatibility of <a href="/wiki/Xbox_360" title="Xbox 360">Xbox 360</a> games on <a href="/wiki/Xbox_One" title="Xbox One">Xbox One</a> console via emulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Also, <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> announced relaunching <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_2" title="PlayStation 2">PS2</a> games on <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_4" title="PlayStation 4">PS4</a> via emulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_aspects">Social aspects</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gamer#Demographics" title="Gamer">Gamer Â§Â Demographics</a></div>
<p>The November 2005 Nielsen Active Gamer Study, taking a survey of 2,000 regular <a href="/wiki/Gamer" title="Gamer">gamers</a>, found that the U.S. games market is diversifying. The age group among male players has expanded significantly in the 25â€“40 age group. For casual online puzzle-style and simple mobile cell phone games, the gender divide is more or less equal between men and women. More recently there has been a growing segment of female players engaged with the aggressive style of games historically considered to fall within traditionally male genres (e.g., <a href="/wiki/First-person_shooter" title="First-person shooter">first-person shooters</a>). According to the ESRB, almost 41% of PC gamers are women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Participation among African-Americans is even lower. One survey of over 2000 game developers returned responses from only 2.5% who identified as black.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Women_and_video_games" title="Women and video games">Women and video games</a></div>
<p>When comparing today's industry climate with that of 20 years ago, women and many adults are more inclined to be using products in the industry. While the market for teen and young adult men is still a strong market, it is the other demographics which are posting significant growth. The <a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Software_Association" title="Entertainment Software Association">Entertainment Software Association</a> (ESA) provides the following summary for 2011 based on a study of almost 1,200 American households carried out by Ipsos MediaCT:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The average gamer is 30 years old and has been playing for 12 years. Eighty-two percent of gamers are 18 years of age or older.</li>
<li>Forty-two percent of all players are women and women over 18 years of age are one of the industry's fastest growing demographics.</li>
<li>Twenty-nine percent of game players are over the age of 50, an increase from nine percent in 1999.</li>
<li>Sixty-five percent of gamers play games with other gamers in person.</li>
<li>Fifty-five percent of gamers play games on their phones or handheld device.</li>
</ul>
<p>A 2006 academic study, based on a survey answered by 10,000 gamers, identified the <a href="/wiki/Gaymer" title="Gaymer">gaymers</a> (gamers that identify as gay) as a demographic group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-first_ever_survey_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-first_ever_survey-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> A follow-up survey in 2009 studied the purchase habits and content preferences of people in the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Based on the study by NPD group in 2011, approximately 91 percent of children aged 2â€“17 play games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Video_game_culture" title="Video game culture">Video game culture</a></div>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Video_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(June 2016)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiplayer">Multiplayer</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multiplayer_video_game" title="Multiplayer video game">Multiplayer video game</a></div>
<p>Video gaming has traditionally been a social experience. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiplayer_video_games" title="Multiplayer video games">Multiplayer video games</a> are those that can be played either competitively, sometimes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_Sports" title="Electronic Sports">Electronic Sports</a>, or cooperatively by using either multiple input devices, or by <a href="/wiki/Hotseat_(multiplayer_mode)" title="Hotseat (multiplayer mode)">hotseating</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Tennis_for_Two" title="Tennis for Two">Tennis for Two</a></i>, arguably the first video game, was a two player game, as was its successor <i><a href="/wiki/Pong" title="Pong">Pong</a></i>. The first commercially available game console, the <a href="/wiki/Magnavox_Odyssey" title="Magnavox Odyssey">Magnavox Odyssey</a>, had two controller inputs.</p>
<p>Since then, most consoles have been shipped with two or four controller inputs. Some have had the ability to expand to four, eight or as many as 12 inputs with additional adapters, such as the <a href="/wiki/Multitap" title="Multitap">Multitap</a>. Multiplayer <a href="/wiki/Arcade_game" title="Arcade game">arcade games</a> typically feature play for two to four players, sometimes tilting the monitor on its back for a top-down viewing experience allowing players to sit opposite one another.</p>
<p>Many early computer games for non-PC descendant based platforms featured multiplayer support. Personal computer systems from <a href="/wiki/Atari" title="Atari">Atari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commodore_International" title="Commodore International">Commodore</a> both regularly featured at least two game ports. PC-based computer games started with a lower availability of multiplayer options because of technical limitations. PCs typically had either one or no game ports at all. Network games for these early personal computers were generally limited to only text based adventures or <a href="/wiki/MUD" title="MUD">MUDs</a> that were played remotely on a dedicated server. This was due both to the slow speed of modems (300-1200-bit/s), and the prohibitive cost involved with putting a computer online in such a way where multiple visitors could make use of it. However, with the advent of widespread <a href="/wiki/Local_area_network" title="Local area network">local area networking</a> technologies and Internet based online capabilities, the number of players in modern games can be 32 or higher, sometimes featuring integrated text and/or voice chat. <a href="/wiki/Massively_multiplayer_online_game" title="Massively multiplayer online game">Massively multiplayer online game</a> (MMOs) can offer extremely high numbers of simultaneous players; <i><a href="/wiki/Eve_Online" title="Eve Online">Eve Online</a></i> set a record with 65,303 players on a single server in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MassivelyEVEPCU_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-MassivelyEVEPCU-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral_effects">Behavioral effects</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_game_behavioral_effects" title="Video game behavioral effects">Video game behavioral effects</a></div>
<p>It has been shown that action video game players have better <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hand%E2%80%93eye_coordination" title="Handâ€“eye coordination">handâ€“eye coordination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motor_coordination" title="Motor coordination">visuo-motor skills</a>, such as their resistance to <a href="/wiki/Distraction" title="Distraction">distraction</a>, their sensitivity to information in the <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_vision" title="Peripheral vision">peripheral vision</a> and their ability to count briefly presented objects, than nonplayers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Researchers found that such enhanced abilities could be acquired by training with action games, involving challenges that switch attention between different locations, but not with games requiring concentration on single objects. It has been suggested by a few studies that online/offline video gaming can be used as a therapeutic tool in the treatment of different mental health concerns.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2009)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steven_Berlin_Johnson" title="Steven Berlin Johnson">Steven Johnson</a>'s book, <i><a href="/wiki/Everything_Bad_Is_Good_for_You" title="Everything Bad Is Good for You">Everything Bad Is Good for You</a></i>, he argues that video games in fact demand far more from a player than traditional games like <i><a href="/wiki/Monopoly_(game)" title="Monopoly (game)">Monopoly</a></i>. To experience the game, the player must first determine the objectives, as well as how to complete them. They must then learn the game controls and how the human-machine interface works, including menus and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HUD_(computer_gaming)" title="HUD (computer gaming)">HUDs</a>. Beyond such skills, which after some time become quite fundamental and are taken for granted by many gamers, video games are based upon the player navigating (and eventually mastering) a highly complex system with many variables. This requires a strong analytical ability, as well as flexibility and adaptability. He argues that the process of learning the boundaries, goals, and controls of a given game is often a highly demanding one that calls on many different areas of cognitive function. Indeed, most games require a great deal of patience and focus from the player, and, contrary to the popular perception that games provide instant gratification, games actually delay gratification far longer than other forms of entertainment such as film or even many books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Some research suggests video games may even increase players' attention capacities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Learning principles found in video games have been identified as possible techniques with which to reform the U.S. education system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> It has been noticed that gamers adopt an attitude while playing that is of such high concentration, they do not realize they are learning, and that if the same attitude could be adopted at school, education would enjoy significant benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Students are found to be "learning by doing" while playing video games while fostering creative thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a> has deployed machines such as the <a href="/wiki/PackBot" title="PackBot">PackBot</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UAV" title="UAV">UAV</a> vehicles, which make use of a game-style <a href="/wiki/Game_controller" title="Game controller">hand controller</a> to make it more familiar for young people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to research discussed at the 2008 Convention of the American Psychological Association, certain types of video games can improve the gamers' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dexterity" title="Dexterity">dexterity</a> as well as their ability to problem-solve. A study of 33 laparoscopic surgeons found that those who played video games were 27 percent faster at advanced surgical procedures and made 37 percent fewer errors compared to those who did not play video games. A second study of 303 laparoscopic surgeons (82 percent men; 18 percent women) also showed that surgeons who played video games requiring spatial skills and hand dexterity and then performed a drill testing these skills were significantly faster at their first attempt and across all 10 trials than the surgeons who did not play the video games first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The research showing benefits from action games has been questioned due to methodological shortcomings, such as recruitment strategies and selection bias, potential placebo effects, and lack of baseline improvements in control groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bootetal_2011_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bootetal_2011-75">[75]</a></sup> In addition, many of the studies are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-sectional" title="Cross-sectional">cross-sectional</a>, and of the <a href="/wiki/Longitudinal_study" title="Longitudinal study">longitudinal</a> interventional trials, not all have found effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bootetal_2011_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bootetal_2011-75">[75]</a></sup> A response to this pointed out that the skill improvements from action games are more broad than predicted, such as <a href="/wiki/Mental_rotation" title="Mental rotation">mental rotation</a>, which is not a common task in action games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Action gamers are not only better at ignoring distractions, but also at focusing on the main task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Objections_to_video_games">Objections to video games</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Video_game_controversies" title="Video game controversies">Video game controversies</a></div>
<p>Like other media, such as <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock music</a> (notably <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">heavy metal music</a>), video games have been the subject of objections, controversies and censorship, for instance because of depictions of violence, sexual themes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcohol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use" title="Recreational drug use">drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a>, profanity or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advergaming" title="Advergaming">advertisements</a>. Critics of video games include parents' groups, politicians, religious groups, scientists and other <a href="/wiki/Advocacy_group" title="Advocacy group">advocacy groups</a>. Claims that some video games cause <a href="/wiki/Video_game_addiction" title="Video game addiction">addiction</a> or violent behavior continue to be made and to be disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>There have been a number of societal and scientific arguments about whether the content of video games change the behavior and attitudes of a player, and whether this is reflected in <a href="/wiki/Video_game_culture" title="Video game culture">video game culture</a> overall. Since the early 1980s, advocates of video games have emphasized their use as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Games_as_art" title="Games as art">expressive medium</a>, arguing for their protection under the laws governing <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a> and also <a href="/wiki/Video_games_in_education" title="Video games in education">as an educational tool</a>. Detractors argue that video games are <a href="/wiki/Harm_principle" title="Harm principle">harmful</a> and therefore should be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_speech_limitations" title="Free speech limitations">subject to legislative oversight and restrictions</a>. The positive and negative characteristics and effects of video games are the subject of scientific study. Results of investigations into links between video games and <a href="/wiki/Video_game_addiction" title="Video game addiction">addiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aggression" title="Aggression">aggression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Violence" title="Violence">violence</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychosocial_development" title="Psychosocial development">social development</a>, and a variety of stereotyping and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexual_morality" title="Sexual morality">sexual morality</a> issues are debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_benefits">Possible benefits</span></h2>
<p>In spite of the negative effects of video games, certain studies indicate that they may have value in terms of academic performance, perhaps because of the skills that are developed in the process. â€œWhen you play ... games youâ€™re solving puzzles to move to the next level and that involves using some of the general knowledge and skills in maths, reading and science that youâ€™ve been taught during the day,â€ said Alberto Posso an Associate Professor at the Royal Melbourne Institute of Technology, after analysing data from the results of standardized testing completed by over 12,000 high school students across Australia. As summarized by <a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> the study [published in the <a href="/wiki/International_Journal_of_Communication" title="International Journal of Communication">International Journal of Communication</a>], "found that students who played online games almost every day scored 15 points above average in maths and reading tests and 17 points above average in science." However, the reporter added an important comment that was not provided by some of the numerous Web sites that published a brief summary of the Australian study study: "[the] methodology cannot prove that playing video games were the cause of the improvement." <a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a> also reported that a Columbia University study indicated that extensive video gaming by students in the 6 to 11 age group provided a greatly increased chance of high intellectual functioning and overall school competence.</p>
<p>In an interview with CNN, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Castronova" title="Edward Castronova">Edward Castronova</a>, a professor of Telecommunications at Indiana University Bloomington said he was not surprised by the outcome of the Australian study but also discussed the issue of causal connection. "Though there is a link between gaming and higher math and science scores, it doesn't mean playing games caused the higher scores. It could just be that kids who are sharp are looking for a challenge, and they don't find it on social media, and maybe they do find it on board games and video games," he explained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ratings_and_censorship">Ratings and censorship</span></h2>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="Globe icon." data-file-height="290" data-file-width="350" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/48px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/73px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/97px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 2x" width="48"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">The examples and perspective in this section <b>deal primarily with Western culture and do not represent a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">worldwide view</a> of the subject</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">You may <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Video_game&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a>, discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Video_game" title="Talk:Video game">talk page</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Article_wizard" title="Wikipedia:Article wizard">create a new article</a>, as appropriate.</span> <small><i>(September 2014)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_game_content_rating_system" title="Video game content rating system">Video game content rating system</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="ESRB">ESRB</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Software_Rating_Board" title="Entertainment Software Rating Board">Entertainment Software Rating Board</a> (ESRB) gives video games maturity ratings based on their content. For example, a game might be rated <i>T</i> for <i>Teen</i> if the game contained obscene words or violence. If a game contains explicit violence or sexual themes, it is likely to receive an <i>M</i> for <i>Mature</i> rating, which means that no one under 17 should play it. There is a rated "A/O" games for "Adults Only" these games have massive violence or nudity. There are no laws that prohibit children from purchasing "M" rated games in the United States. Laws attempting to prohibit minors from purchasing "M" rated games were established in California, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, but all were overturned on the grounds that these laws violated the First Amendment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-legal_results_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-legal_results-82">[82]</a></sup> However, many stores have opted to not sell such games to children anyway. Of course, video game laws vary from country to country. One of the most controversial games of all time, <i><a href="/wiki/Manhunt_2" title="Manhunt 2">Manhunt 2</a></i> by Rockstar Studios, was given an <a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Software_Rating_Board#Restricted_ratings" title="Entertainment Software Rating Board">AO rating</a> by the ESRB until Rockstar could make the content more suitable for a mature audience.</p>
<p>Video game manufacturers usually exercise tight control over the games that are made available on their systems, so unusual or special-interest games are more likely to appear as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PC_games" title="PC games">PC games</a>. Free, casual, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Browser-based_game" title="Browser-based game">browser-based games</a> are usually played on available computers, mobile phones, or <a href="/wiki/Personal_digital_assistant" title="Personal digital assistant">PDAs</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="PEGI">PEGI</span></h3>
